inline-share-btn-label-linkedin\">LINKEDIN</span></a><span\n            class=\"inline-share-btn inline-share-btn-comments\"\n            data-share-method=\"comments\"\n            data-uotrack=\"InlineShareCommentsBtn\"><span class=\"inline-share-count inline-share-count-comments\"></span><span class=\"inline-share-btn-label inline-share-btn-label-comments\">COMMENT</span></span><span\n        class=\"inline-share-btn inline-share-btn-email\"\n        data-share-method=\"email\"\n        data-uotrack=\"InlineShareEmailBtn\"><span class=\"inline-share-count inline-share-count-email\"></span><span class=\"inline-share-btn-label inline-share-btn-label-email\">EMAIL</span></span><span\n         class=\"inline-share-btn inline-share-btn-more\"\n         data-share-method=\"facebook\"\n         data-uotrack=\"InlineShareMoreBtn\"><span class=\"inline-share-count inline-share-count-more\"></span><span class=\"inline-share-btn-label inline-share-btn-label-more\">MORE</span></span></div></div><p>Workers in moon suits stand at\u00a0the edge of a movable platform, carefully angling 12-foot high assemblies of fuel rods stocked with uranium\u00a0into the depths of a cooling pool that will double as a radiation\u00a0shield.</p><p>Each assembly costs $1 million, holds 204 fuel rods and 49,000 uranium pellets, each pellet the energy-producing equivalent of a ton of coal.</p><p>As workers position the assemblies with\u00a0a hand-controlled pulley, a blue glow comes over the water, what happens when electrically-charged particles move through water\u00a0faster than the speed of light.\u00a0It\u2019s called Cherenkov\u2019s radiation for the Russian scientist who discovered it back in 1934 and has been likened to the sonic boom that occurs when a plane exceeds the speed of sound.</p><p><strong><span class=\"exclude-from-newsgate\"><a href=\"/story/news/investigations/2017/02/02/bedford-mamaroneck-north-salem-nuclear-bailout/97137838/\">TOWNS: Bedford, Mamaroneck, North Salem challenge nuke plant bailout</a></span></strong></p><p><span class=\"exclude-from-newsgate\"><strong><a href=\"/story/news/investigations/2016/10/12/nuclear-plants-bailout/91347458/\">HIKES: Electric bills going up $2 to bail out upstate plants</a></strong></span></p><p><span class=\"exclude-from-newsgate\"><strong><a href=\"/story/news/investigations/2017/01/13/indian-point-power/96442628/\">CLOSING: After Indian Point, where will we get electricity</a></strong></span></p><p>Entergy agreed to let a reporter and photographer from The Journal News/lohud\u00a0inside the power plant to witness the early stages of a refueling last week. The tour provided a rare glimpse\u00a0inside a working\u00a0nuclear reactor, one that was in operation for 453 consecutive days since its last refueling.</p><p>The fuel rod transfer\u00a0is\u00a0the main event in a well-choreographed, $100 million refueling that has become an annual rite of spring at the Indian Point Nuclear Power Plant. The used and spent fuel assemblies will soon take their place beside hundreds of others, remnants of four decades\u2019 worth of energy to homes in Westchester County and New York City.</p><p>\u201cWe are investing tens of millions of dollars during this refueling and maintenance outage on inspections, maintenance, and equipment upgrades to ensure the plant continues to operate safely and provide economic stimulus,\u201d said Tony Vitale, the top-ranking official at Indian Point for its owner, Louisiana-based Entergy.</p><h2 class=\"presto-h2\">Economy will miss this</h2><p>With Indian Point slated to close\u00a0in 2021, there are just two more refueling events scheduled for the power plant\u00a0along the shores of the Hudson River in the Westchester County village of Buchanan.</p><p>Timed to coincide with a time of year when energy consumption is at a low, the refueling has become a much-anticipated event, and not just for the work that goes on inside the reactor.</p><p>Local businesses have come to rely on the\u00a0refuelings for an annual infusion of cash. And they are not too pleased that a reliable revenue stream is about to be choked off.</p><p>\u201cIt\u2019s bad news all around,\u201d said Peekskill Mayor Frank Catalina. \u201cHow bad depends on the company. It\u2019s just a terrible situation.\u201d</p><p>Entergy announced in January that it would shut down operations at Indian Point, an outcome long sought by Gov. Andrew Cuomo, who questioned whether the densely-populated towns and cities near the plant could be evacuated in the event of a mishap.</p><p>Westchester County Executive Rob Astorino criticized Cuomo for moving ahead with a plan to shut down Indian Point without considering the economic fallout for the region.</p><div id=\"module-position-P1tNsHzHJKs\" class=\"story-asset image-asset\"><aside itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"wide single-photo\"><img itemprop=\"url\" src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/35d62ae2686a70040807f2d77242d85c22a59408/c=1838-1112-4676-3246&amp;r=x408&amp;c=540x405/local/-/media/2017/02/28/Westchester/Westchester/636238758118266856-102.JPG\" alt=\"Westchester County Executive Rob Astorino faced an\" width=\"540\" height=\"405\" data-mycapture-src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/media/2017/02/28/Westchester/Westchester/636238758118266856-102.JPG\"\n                    data-mycapture-sm-src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/521cd80a4fb9f1f1edf37d025629b379daee5bba/r=500x332/local/-/media/2017/02/28/Westchester/Westchester/636238758118266856-102.JPG\" /><meta itemprop=\"name\" content=\"Rob Astorino town hall\" /><meta itemprop=\"height\" content=\"405\" /><meta itemprop=\"width\" content=\"540\" /><span class=\"mycapture-btn-wrap\"><span class=\"mycapture-non-priority-horizontal-image mycapture-btn-with-text js-mycapture-btn\">Buy Photo</span></span><p>Westchester County Executive Rob Astorino faced an impassioned audience in White Plains, Monday, Feb. 27, 2017.<meta itemprop=\"copyrightHolder\" content=\"David McKay Wilson/The Journal News\" />&nbsp;<span class=\"credit\">(Photo: David McKay Wilson/The Journal News)</span></p></aside><div class=\"clearfix\"></div></div><p>\"The governor is taking away\u00a0more than 1,000 high-paying jobs, more than $30 million in local tax revenues generated by Indian Point, and more than $1.3 billion in local economic output,\" Astorino said. \"This plan to close Indian Point puts the economic and environmental future of our region at risk.\u201d</p><h2 class=\"presto-h2\">Like a Super Bowl\u00a0</h2><p>The refueling will recharge\u00a0Unit 3,\u00a0one of\u00a0Indian Point\u2019s two working reactors, shutting it down while\u00a0technicians drain water and inspect\u00a0for deficiencies like worn bolts and, when\u00a0that\u2019s done, inserting enough new fuel assemblies to keep the reactor going\u00a0another two years.</p><p>For the five or six weeks the project is underway, the towns around Indian Point get their own jolt, in the form of economic activity.\u00a0Think the Super Bowl, The Master\u2019s\u00a0or the National Collegiate Athletic Association's (NCAA) \u00a0Final Four\u00a0coming to town every year, only on a smaller scale.</p><div id=\"module-position-P1tNsHyk6eA\" class=\"story-asset image-asset\"><aside itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"wide single-photo\"><img itemprop=\"url\" src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/4b4285c1d64d50de5d52d9f73b4d2a8eb66f33a6/c=326-0-5433-3840&amp;r=x408&amp;c=540x405/local/-/media/2017/03/21/Westchester/Westchester/636257051487378494-rf03202017ip3029.jpg\" alt=\"Contractors work on the steam turbines during a routine\" width=\"540\" height=\"405\" data-mycapture-src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/media/2017/03/21/Westchester/Westchester/636257051487378494-rf03202017ip3029.jpg\"\n                    data-mycapture-sm-src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/4044b1e7645488621f157de466cf1a990870caa2/r=500x333/local/-/media/2017/03/21/Westchester/Westchester/636257051487378494-rf03202017ip3029.jpg\" /><meta itemprop=\"name\" content=\"Indian Point 3 Entergy\" /><meta itemprop=\"height\" content=\"405\" /><meta itemprop=\"width\" content=\"540\" /><span class=\"mycapture-btn-wrap\"><span class=\"mycapture-non-priority-horizontal-image mycapture-btn-with-text js-mycapture-btn\">Buy Photo</span></span><p>Contractors work on the steam turbines during a routine maintenance of Indian Point 3 at the Energy Indian Point Energy Center in Buchanan on Mar. 20, 2017. <meta itemprop=\"copyrightHolder\" content=\"Ricky Flores/The Journal News\" />&nbsp;<span class=\"credit\">(Photo: Ricky Flores/The Journal News)</span></p></aside><div class=\"clearfix\"></div></div><p>When the reactor shuts down, Indian Point\u2019s 1,000-person workforce doubles as an army of out-of-state workers \u2014 carpenters, electricians, steamfitters, ironworkers and others \u2014 floods into the lower Hudson Valley.</p><p>Restaurants, hotels, shops all see\u00a0a bump in revenue.</p><p>The landmark Inn on the Hudson at Peekskill, formerly The Peekskill Inn, receives between 10 and 15 percent of its annual income from Indian Point-related business, offered general manager Doug DelliPaoli. A good chunk of that comes during March and April when the inn\u2019s 53 rooms are booked.</p><p>\u201cIt hurts,\u201d said DelliPaoli. \u201cI don\u2019t care how much you\u2019re making. It\u2019s going to hurt and we\u2019re going to have to replace it.\u201d</p><h2 class=\"presto-h2\">No science fiction flick</h2><p>To get to the cooling pool, you first pass through a phalanx of security worthy of a maximum security prison.</p><p>Along the way there are a series of revolving steel doors, armed security guards, radiation detectors and a science lesson led by Patrick Falciano, who\u2019s been working at Indian Point in one job or another for nearly 50 years.</p><p>Falciano describes the difference between radiation and radiological material \u2014 dirt or dust. It\u2019s a point he will come back to again and again as he tries to rid us of any ideas about radiation we may have taken away from science fiction movies.</p><p>Radiation is energy, Falciano explains. It doesn\u2019t make people glow a bright green, Falciano says.</p><p>\u201cIt\u2019s not the radiation that can be transferred because that\u2019s just energy,\u201d Falciano says. \u201cBut the physical material that\u2019s giving up that energy can be transferred like anything else.\u201d</p><p>If a workers\u2019 hands come in contact with radiological material he or she washes his hand in a sink. And not just any old sink but one that leads to a basin that will boil down the water and parse out radiological residue so it doesn\u2019t enter the water supply.</p><p>\u201cIt\u2019s just like a person who does a dirty job anywhere,\u201d Falciano says. \u201cIf a mechanic works on a car, or a painter paints a house, you put on a pair of overalls. And why do they do that? To keep the dirt off their own skin or their own clothing.\u201d</p><p>He warns us to keep our hands to ourselves during the time we\u2019re inside the reactor, except when descending stairs, when we\u2019ll need to hold onto handrails.</p><p>\u201cThat\u2019s the only thing I want you to touch,\u201d Falciano says.</p><div id=\"module-position-P1tNsHzjUxM\" class=\"story-asset image-asset\"><aside itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"wide single-photo\"><img itemprop=\"url\" src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/4b4285c1d64d50de5d52d9f73b4d2a8eb66f33a6/c=326-0-5433-3840&amp;r=x408&amp;c=540x405/local/-/media/2017/03/21/Westchester/Westchester/636257051982684844-rf03202017ip3050.jpg\" alt=\"The access control point for Indian Point 3 where workers\" width=\"540\" height=\"405\" data-mycapture-src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/media/2017/03/21/Westchester/Westchester/636257051982684844-rf03202017ip3050.jpg\"\n                    data-mycapture-sm-src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/4044b1e7645488621f157de466cf1a990870caa2/r=500x333/local/-/media/2017/03/21/Westchester/Westchester/636257051982684844-rf03202017ip3050.jpg\" /><meta itemprop=\"name\" content=\"Indian Point 3 Entergy\" /><meta itemprop=\"height\" content=\"405\" /><meta itemprop=\"width\" content=\"540\" /><span class=\"mycapture-btn-wrap\"><span class=\"mycapture-non-priority-horizontal-image mycapture-btn-with-text js-mycapture-btn\">Buy Photo</span></span><p>The access control point for Indian Point 3 where workers are equipped with dosimeters and are check out for radiation exposure when exiting the containment building at the Energy Indian Point Energy Center in Buchanan on Mar. 20, 2017. <meta itemprop=\"copyrightHolder\" content=\"Ricky Flores/The Journal News\" />&nbsp;<span class=\"credit\">(Photo: Ricky Flores/The Journal News)</span></p></aside><div class=\"clearfix\"></div></div><p>We\u2019ll wear a digital dosimeter attached to a lanyard, which will let us know how much radiation we\u2019ve been exposed to during our stay. Very often, it\u2019s an amount less than what we\u2019d be exposed to by a dental X-ray, Falciano says.</p><p>At a radiation checkpoint, dozens of workers come and go, many in hospital scrubs, stopping briefly at computers to see whether their dosimeters have picked up any detectable levels of radiation. They\u2019ll need to pass through a radiation detector before they go back inside the reactor.</p><p>Just beyond the checkpoint, up a flight of stairs, is the cooling pool and a heavily fortified, glass-enclosed room where each of the fuel assemblies, glistening like gold ingot, are stored until they\u2019re put to work in the reactor. They were trucked here by flatbed in the weeks ahead of the refueling.</p><div id=\"module-position-P1tNsHzn30U\" class=\"story-asset image-asset\"><aside itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"wide single-photo\"><img itemprop=\"url\" src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/43b0aedf3ba571fb47922a16d83621fcc5b20658/c=294-0-4890-3456&amp;r=x408&amp;c=540x405/local/-/media/2017/03/21/Westchester/Westchester/636257051000340250-rf03202017ip3009.jpg\" alt=\"New fuel rod containers wait to be installed  into\" width=\"540\" height=\"405\" data-mycapture-src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/media/2017/03/21/Westchester/Westchester/636257051000340250-rf03202017ip3009.jpg\"\n                    data-mycapture-sm-src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/4044b1e7645488621f157de466cf1a990870caa2/r=500x333/local/-/media/2017/03/21/Westchester/Westchester/636257051000340250-rf03202017ip3009.jpg\" /><meta itemprop=\"name\" content=\"Indian Point 3 Entergy\" /><meta itemprop=\"height\" content=\"405\" /><meta itemprop=\"width\" content=\"540\" /><span class=\"mycapture-btn-wrap\"><span class=\"mycapture-non-priority-horizontal-image mycapture-btn-with-text js-mycapture-btn\">Buy Photo</span></span><p>New fuel rod containers wait to be installed  into Indian Point 3 at the Energy Indian Point Energy Center in Buchanan on Mar. 20, 2017. <meta itemprop=\"copyrightHolder\" content=\"Ricky Flores/The Journal News\" />&nbsp;<span class=\"credit\">(Photo: Ricky Flores/The Journal News)</span></p></aside><div class=\"clearfix\"></div></div><h2 class=\"presto-h2\">Rolling stones on nuke trail\u00a0</h2><p>Workers sport T-shirts and hardhats that hint at the itinerant life of the nuclear power plant worker.</p><p>\u201cHope Creek Generating Station 2016,\u201d reads a T-shirt from the New Jersey nuclear power plant. \u201cPilgrim 2015,\u201d reads a sticker on a hardhat for the Pilgrim Nuclear Power Station in Plymouth, Massachusetts.</p><p>There are carpenters from Virginia and Ohio, a nuclear engineer from Pittsburgh and painters from rural parts of Pennsylvania. While they\u2019re here, they\u2019ll work 12-hour shifts six or seven days a week.</p><p><span class=\"exclude-from-newsgate\"><strong><a href=\"/story/news/investigations/2016/05/18/indian-point-fitzpatrick/83981086/\">BATTLE: Shutdown battle: A tale of 2 nuclear power plants</a></strong></span></p><p><span class=\"exclude-from-newsgate\"><strong><a href=\"/story/insider/2017/02/22/ny-solar-power-race-sun/97295562/\">SOLAR: NY solar power: The race for the sun</a></strong></span></p><p><span class=\"exclude-from-newsgate\"><strong><a href=\"/story/news/local/indian-point/2017/01/09/entergy-litigation-indian-point-shutdown/96352404/\">ENTERGY: Costly litigation a factor in Indian Point shutdown decision</a></strong></span></p><div id=\"module-position-P1tNsHy8lvs\" class=\"story-asset image-asset\"><aside itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"wide single-photo\"><img itemprop=\"url\" src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/43b0aedf3ba571fb47922a16d83621fcc5b20658/c=294-0-4890-3456&amp;r=x408&amp;c=540x405/local/-/media/2017/03/29/Westchester/Westchester/636264007226639171-rf03202017ip3004.jpg\" alt=\"Serina Snyder, a radiation protection technician, is\" width=\"540\" height=\"405\" data-mycapture-src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/media/2017/03/29/Westchester/Westchester/636264007226639171-rf03202017ip3004.jpg\"\n                    data-mycapture-sm-src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/4044b1e7645488621f157de466cf1a990870caa2/r=500x333/local/-/media/2017/03/29/Westchester/Westchester/636264007226639171-rf03202017ip3004.jpg\" /><meta itemprop=\"name\" content=\"Serina Snyder\" /><meta itemprop=\"height\" content=\"405\" /><meta itemprop=\"width\" content=\"540\" /><span class=\"mycapture-btn-wrap\"><span class=\"mycapture-non-priority-horizontal-image mycapture-btn-with-text js-mycapture-btn\">Buy Photo</span></span><p>Serina Snyder, a radiation protection technician, is monitoring radiation levels that may expose workers while maintenance work is being done at Indian Point 3 at the Entergy Indian Point Energy Center in Buchanan on Mar. 20, 2017. <meta itemprop=\"copyrightHolder\" content=\"Ricky Flores/The Journal News\" />&nbsp;<span class=\"credit\">(Photo: Ricky Flores/The Journal News)</span></p></aside><div class=\"clearfix\"></div></div><p>Serina Snyder has gotten used to seeing a lot of the same faces during her 14 years of travelling to nuclear facilities, many from upstate Oswego County where she lives.</p><p>Snyder spends most of her time at the James A. FitzPatrick Nuclear Power Station in Oswego working as\u00a0a radiation protection technician, making sure workers are dressed properly and not unnecessarily exposed to radiological material.</p><div id=\"module-position-P1tNsHz3hVY\" class=\"story-asset image-asset\"><aside itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"wide single-photo\"><img itemprop=\"url\" src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/c28f140d44aea85808df069d7b53b30b523a2341/c=581-0-5177-3456&amp;r=x408&amp;c=540x405/local/-/media/2017/03/29/Westchester/Westchester/636264007246139546-rf03202017ip3005.jpg\" alt=\"Serina Snyder, a radiation protection technician, is\" width=\"540\" height=\"405\" data-mycapture-src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/media/2017/03/29/Westchester/Westchester/636264007246139546-rf03202017ip3005.jpg\"\n                    data-mycapture-sm-src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/4044b1e7645488621f157de466cf1a990870caa2/r=500x333/local/-/media/2017/03/29/Westchester/Westchester/636264007246139546-rf03202017ip3005.jpg\" /><meta itemprop=\"name\" content=\"Serina Snyder\" /><meta itemprop=\"height\" content=\"405\" /><meta itemprop=\"width\" content=\"540\" /><span class=\"mycapture-btn-wrap\"><span class=\"mycapture-non-priority-horizontal-image mycapture-btn-with-text js-mycapture-btn\">Buy Photo</span></span><p>Serina Snyder, a radiation protection technician, is monitoring radiation levels that may expose workers while maintenance work is being done at Indian Point 3 at the Entergy Indian Point Energy Center in Buchanan on Mar. 20, 2017. <meta itemprop=\"copyrightHolder\" content=\"Ricky Flores/The Journal News\" />&nbsp;<span class=\"credit\">(Photo: Ricky Flores/The Journal News)</span></p></aside><div class=\"clearfix\"></div></div><p>But, since graduating college with a chemistry degree, she\u2019s done contracting work at most of Entergy\u2019s plants in the U.S, in Louisiana, Michigan and Arkansas. She ticks them off like she\u2019s reading a grocery list.</p><p>\u201cI\u2019ve been to all the northern plants,\u201d she said. \u201cVermont Yankee, Pilgrim, Indian Point, FitzPatrick, Nine Mile. I\u2019ve been to Palisades once and I\u2019ve been to Waterford, River Bend.\u201d</p><p>When in town, she likes to stay in quick commuting distance to Indian Point. \u201cI normally stay in Fishkill, something where I can cook so I don\u2019t have to eat out all the time,\u201d Snyder said.</p><p>In her down time, she hunts for bargains.</p><p>\u201cI usually come down here and go to Woodbury Commons,\u201d she said. \u201cWhen I\u2019m here, every outage I go shopping.\u201d</p><p>The loss of Snyder\u2019s shopping forays will be just a small fraction of the revenue lost once Indian Point shuts down.</p><p>A 2012 study by the Business Council of Westchester estimated that the plant\u2019s total payroll is $130 million. It paid another $75 million in annual property taxes and made charitable contributions of $2 million that poured into local communities.</p><div id=\"module-position-P1tNsHzAnnU\" class=\"story-asset image-asset\"><aside itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"wide single-photo\"><img itemprop=\"url\" src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/4b4285c1d64d50de5d52d9f73b4d2a8eb66f33a6/c=326-0-5433-3840&amp;r=x408&amp;c=540x405/local/-/media/2017/03/21/Westchester/Westchester/636257051550871308-rf03202017ip3032.jpg\" alt=\"Contractors work on the steam turbines during a routine\" width=\"540\" height=\"405\" data-mycapture-src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/media/2017/03/21/Westchester/Westchester/636257051550871308-rf03202017ip3032.jpg\"\n                    data-mycapture-sm-src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/4044b1e7645488621f157de466cf1a990870caa2/r=500x333/local/-/media/2017/03/21/Westchester/Westchester/636257051550871308-rf03202017ip3032.jpg\" /><meta itemprop=\"name\" content=\"Indian Point 3 Entergy\" /><meta itemprop=\"height\" content=\"405\" /><meta itemprop=\"width\" content=\"540\" /><span class=\"mycapture-btn-wrap\"><span class=\"mycapture-non-priority-horizontal-image mycapture-btn-with-text js-mycapture-btn\">Buy Photo</span></span><p>Contractors work on the steam turbines during a routine maintenance of Indian Point 3 at the Energy Indian Point Energy Center in Buchanan on Mar. 20, 2017. <meta itemprop=\"copyrightHolder\" content=\"Ricky Flores/The Journal News\" />&nbsp;<span class=\"credit\">(Photo: Ricky Flores/The Journal News)</span></p></aside><div class=\"clearfix\"></div></div><p>Nearby municipalities estimate they could lose $32 million. The village of Buchanan predicts it could lose $3.5 million annually, nearly half its budget.</p><p>The Hendrick Hudson School District could be left with a $20 million hole in its $75 million budget in the coming years. The district currently receives a $23.3 million Payment in Lieu of Taxes (PILOT). But the PILOT payment will be reduced to $2.3 million three years after Indian Point shuts down.</p><p>And it\u2019s unclear if Buchanan will ever be able to depend on Indian Point\u2019s 240-acres to generate property taxes. That\u2019s because those fuel rods from the cooling pool will be moved to dry casks on the property after the shut down. Currently, there\u2019s nowhere else for them to go.</p><p>In late February, Cuomo said he would create a task force of state and elected officials to come up with a plan to help towns that have\u00a0relied on Indian Point for their economic lifeline.</p><p>\u201cWe\u2019re going to lose a lot of high-paying jobs and property taxes,\u201d said John Ravitz, the executive vice president of the Business Council. \u201cThere\u2019s a whole host of issues that we are dealing with and our message to the governor is \u2018OK, you got your wish, now what?\u201d</p><div Point: Rare look inside a working reactor", "url": "http://www.lohud.com/story/news/investigations/2017/03/30/indian-point-working-reactor/99458156/", "date": "03-30-2017", "source": "usa-today", "text": "CLOSE Skip in Skip x Embed x Share Work amounting to $100 million is being done on Indian Point 3 nuclear reactor as part of a routine 2 year maintenance cycle that last about 6 weeks. Ricky Flores/lohud\n\nEntergy gave The Journal News/lohud.com a rare peek inside a nuclear power plant during a refueling of its Unit 3 reactor\n\nBuy Photo Work to replace the fuel rods at Indian Point 3 as well as the replacement, refurbishment and testing of equipment is underway as part of routine maintenance of the nuclear reactor in Buchanan on Mar. 20, 2017. (Photo: Ricky Flores/The Journal News)Buy Photo Story Highlights Businesses near Indian Point make a sizable chunk of their annual income from refuelings at the power plant.\n\nIndian Point's workforce of 1,000 doubles during the six-week refueling period\n\nHotels, restaurants, pizza places and construction businesses are expected to lose revenue\n\nWorkers in moon suits stand at the edge of a movable platform, carefully angling 12-foot high assemblies of fuel rods stocked with uranium into the depths of a cooling pool that will double as a radiation shield.\n\nEach assembly costs $1 million, holds 204 fuel rods and 49,000 uranium pellets, each pellet the energy-producing equivalent of a ton of coal.\n\nAs workers position the assemblies with a hand-controlled pulley, a blue glow comes over the water, what happens when electrically-charged particles move through water faster than the speed of light. It\u2019s called Cherenkov\u2019s radiation for the Russian scientist who discovered it back in 1934 and has been likened to the sonic boom that occurs when a plane exceeds the speed of sound.\n\nTOWNS: Bedford, Mamaroneck, North Salem challenge nuke plant bailout\n\nHIKES: Electric bills going up $2 to bail out upstate plants\n\nCLOSING: After Indian Point, where will we get electricity\n\nEntergy agreed to let a reporter and photographer from The Journal News/lohud inside the power plant to witness the early stages of a refueling last week. The tour provided a rare glimpse inside a working nuclear reactor, one that was in operation for 453 consecutive days since its last refueling.\n\nThe fuel rod transfer is the main event in a well-choreographed, $100 million refueling that has become an annual rite of spring at the Indian Point Nuclear Power Plant. The used and spent fuel assemblies will soon take their place beside hundreds of others, remnants of four decades\u2019 worth of energy to homes in Westchester County and New York City.\n\n\u201cWe are investing tens of millions of dollars during this refueling and maintenance outage on inspections, maintenance, and equipment upgrades to ensure the plant continues to operate safely and provide economic stimulus,\u201d said Tony Vitale, the top-ranking official at Indian Point for its owner, Louisiana-based Entergy.\n\nEconomy will miss this\n\nWith Indian Point slated to close in 2021, there are just two more refueling events scheduled for the power plant along the shores of the Hudson River in the Westchester County village of Buchanan.\n\nTimed to coincide with a time of year when energy consumption is at a low, the refueling has become a much-anticipated event, and not just for the work that goes on inside the reactor.\n\nLocal businesses have come to rely on the refuelings for an annual infusion of cash. And they are not too pleased that a reliable revenue stream is about to be choked off.\n\n\u201cIt\u2019s bad news all around,\u201d said Peekskill Mayor Frank Catalina. \u201cHow bad depends on the company. It\u2019s just a terrible situation.\u201d\n\nEntergy announced in January that it would shut down operations at Indian Point, an outcome long sought by Gov. Andrew Cuomo, who questioned whether the densely-populated towns and cities near the plant could be evacuated in the event of a mishap.\n\nWestchester County Executive Rob Astorino criticized Cuomo for moving ahead with a plan to shut down Indian Point without considering the economic fallout for the region.\n\nBuy Photo Westchester County Executive Rob Astorino faced an impassioned audience in White Plains, Monday, Feb. 27, 2017. (Photo: David McKay Wilson/The Journal News)\n\n\"The governor is taking away more than 1,000 high-paying jobs, more than $30 million in local tax revenues generated by Indian Point, and more than $1.3 billion in local economic output,\" Astorino said. \"This plan to close Indian Point puts the economic and environmental future of our region at risk.\u201d\n\nLike a Super Bowl\n\nThe refueling will recharge Unit 3, one of Indian Point\u2019s two working reactors, shutting it down while technicians drain water and inspect for deficiencies like worn bolts and, when that\u2019s done, inserting enough new fuel assemblies to keep the reactor going another two years.\n\nFor the five or six weeks the project is underway, the towns around Indian Point get their own jolt, in the form of economic activity. Think the Super Bowl, The Master\u2019s or the National Collegiate Athletic Association's (NCAA) Final Four coming to town every year, only on a smaller scale.\n\nBuy Photo Contractors work on the steam turbines during a routine maintenance of Indian Point 3 at the Energy Indian Point Energy Center in Buchanan on Mar. 20, 2017. (Photo: Ricky Flores/The Journal News)\n\nWhen the reactor shuts down, Indian Point\u2019s 1,000-person workforce doubles as an army of out-of-state workers \u2014 carpenters, electricians, steamfitters, ironworkers and others \u2014 floods into the lower Hudson Valley.\n\nRestaurants, hotels, shops all see a bump in revenue.\n\nThe landmark Inn on the Hudson at Peekskill, formerly The Peekskill Inn, receives between 10 and 15 percent of its annual income from Indian Point-related business, offered general manager Doug DelliPaoli. A good chunk of that comes during March and April when the inn\u2019s 53 rooms are booked.\n\n\u201cIt hurts,\u201d said DelliPaoli. \u201cI don\u2019t care how much you\u2019re making. It\u2019s going to hurt and we\u2019re going to have to replace it.\u201d\n\nNo science fiction flick\n\nTo get to the cooling pool, you first pass through a phalanx of security worthy of a maximum security prison.\n\nAlong the way there are a series of revolving steel doors, armed security guards, radiation detectors and a science lesson led by Patrick Falciano, who\u2019s been working at Indian Point in one job or another for nearly 50 years.\n\nFalciano describes the difference between radiation and radiological material \u2014 dirt or dust. It\u2019s a point he will come back to again and again as he tries to rid us of any ideas about radiation we may have taken away from science fiction movies.\n\nRadiation is energy, Falciano explains. It doesn\u2019t make people glow a bright green, Falciano says.\n\n\u201cIt\u2019s not the radiation that can be transferred because that\u2019s just energy,\u201d Falciano says. \u201cBut the physical material that\u2019s giving up that energy can be transferred like anything else.\u201d\n\nIf a workers\u2019 hands come in contact with radiological material he or she washes his hand in a sink. And not just any old sink but one that leads to a basin that will boil down the water and parse out radiological residue so it doesn\u2019t enter the water supply.\n\n\u201cIt\u2019s just like a person who does a dirty job anywhere,\u201d Falciano says. \u201cIf a mechanic works on a car, or a painter paints a house, you put on a pair of overalls. And why do they do that? To keep the dirt off their own skin or their own clothing.\u201d\n\nHe warns us to keep our hands to ourselves during the time we\u2019re inside the reactor, except when descending stairs, when we\u2019ll need to hold onto handrails.\n\n\u201cThat\u2019s the only thing I want you to touch,\u201d Falciano says.\n\nBuy Photo The access control point for Indian Point 3 where workers are equipped with dosimeters and are check out for radiation exposure when exiting the containment building at the Energy Indian Point Energy Center in Buchanan on Mar. 20, 2017. (Photo: Ricky Flores/The Journal News)\n\nWe\u2019ll wear a digital dosimeter attached to a lanyard, which will let us know how much radiation we\u2019ve been exposed to during our stay. Very often, it\u2019s an amount less than what we\u2019d be exposed to by a dental X-ray, Falciano says.\n\nAt a radiation checkpoint, dozens of workers come and go, many in hospital scrubs, stopping briefly at computers to see whether their dosimeters have picked up any detectable levels of radiation. They\u2019ll need to pass through a radiation detector before they go back inside the reactor.\n\nJust beyond the checkpoint, up a flight of stairs, is the cooling pool and a heavily fortified, glass-enclosed room where each of the fuel assemblies, glistening like gold ingot, are stored until they\u2019re put to work in the reactor. They were trucked here by flatbed in the weeks ahead of the refueling.\n\nBuy Photo New fuel rod containers wait to be installed into Indian Point 3 at the Energy Indian Point Energy Center in Buchanan on Mar. 20, 2017. (Photo: Ricky Flores/The Journal News)\n\nRolling stones on nuke trail\n\nWorkers sport T-shirts and hardhats that hint at the itinerant life of the nuclear power plant worker.\n\n\u201cHope Creek Generating Station 2016,\u201d reads a T-shirt from the New Jersey nuclear power plant. \u201cPilgrim 2015,\u201d reads a sticker on a hardhat for the Pilgrim Nuclear Power Station in Plymouth, Massachusetts.\n\nThere are carpenters from Virginia and Ohio, a nuclear engineer from Pittsburgh and painters from rural parts of Pennsylvania. While they\u2019re here, they\u2019ll work 12-hour shifts six or seven days a week.\n\nBATTLE: Shutdown battle: A tale of 2 nuclear power plants\n\nSOLAR: NY solar power: The race for the sun\n\nENTERGY: Costly litigation a factor in Indian Point shutdown decision\n\nBuy Photo Serina Snyder, a radiation protection technician, is monitoring radiation levels that may expose workers while maintenance work is being done at Indian Point 3 at the Entergy Indian Point Energy Center in Buchanan on Mar. 20, 2017. (Photo: Ricky Flores/The Journal News)\n\nSerina Snyder has gotten used to seeing a lot of the same faces during her 14 years of travelling to nuclear facilities, many from upstate Oswego County where she lives.\n\nSnyder spends most of her time at the James A. FitzPatrick Nuclear Power Station in Oswego working as a radiation protection technician, making sure workers are dressed properly and not unnecessarily exposed to radiological material.\n\nBuy Photo Serina Snyder, a radiation protection technician, is monitoring radiation levels that may expose workers while maintenance work is being done at Indian Point 3 at the Entergy Indian Point Energy Center in Buchanan on Mar. 20, 2017. (Photo: Ricky Flores/The Journal News)\n\nBut, since graduating college with a chemistry degree, she\u2019s done contracting work at most of Entergy\u2019s plants in the U.S, in Louisiana, Michigan and Arkansas. She ticks them off like she\u2019s reading a grocery list.\n\n\u201cI\u2019ve been to all the northern plants,\u201d she said. \u201cVermont Yankee, Pilgrim, Indian Point, FitzPatrick, Nine Mile. I\u2019ve been to Palisades once and I\u2019ve been to Waterford, River Bend.\u201d\n\nWhen in town, she likes to stay in quick commuting distance to Indian Point. \u201cI normally stay in Fishkill, something where I can cook so I don\u2019t have to eat out all the time,\u201d Snyder said.\n\nIn her down time, she hunts for bargains.\n\n\u201cI usually come down here and go to Woodbury Commons,\u201d she said. \u201cWhen I\u2019m here, every outage I go shopping.\u201d\n\nThe loss of Snyder\u2019s shopping forays will be just a small fraction of the revenue lost once Indian Point shuts down.\n\nA 2012 study by the Business Council of Westchester estimated that the plant\u2019s total payroll is $130 million. It paid another $75 million in annual property taxes and made charitable contributions of $2 million that poured into local communities.\n\nBuy Photo Contractors work on the steam turbines during a routine maintenance of Indian Point 3 at the Energy Indian Point Energy Center in Buchanan on Mar. 20, 2017. (Photo: Ricky Flores/The Journal News)\n\nNearby municipalities estimate they could lose $32 million. The village of Buchanan predicts it could lose $3.5 million annually, nearly half its budget.\n\nThe Hendrick Hudson School District could be left with a $20 million hole in its $75 million budget in the coming years. The district currently receives a $23.3 million Payment in Lieu of Taxes (PILOT). But the PILOT payment will be reduced to $2.3 million three years after Indian Point shuts down.\n\nAnd it\u2019s unclear if Buchanan will ever be able to depend on Indian Point\u2019s 240-acres to generate property taxes. That\u2019s because those fuel rods from the cooling pool will be moved to dry casks on the property after the shut down. Currently, there\u2019s nowhere else for them to go.\n\nIn late February, Cuomo said he would create a task force of state and elected officials to come up with a plan to help towns that have relied on Indian Point for their economic lifeline.\n\n\u201cWe\u2019re going to lose a lot of high-paying jobs and property taxes,\u201d said John Ravitz, the executive vice president of the Business Council. \u201cThere\u2019s a whole host of issues that we are dealing with and our message to the governor is \u2018OK, you got your wish, now what?\u201d\n\nRead or Share this story:
